ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Physical_cosmology" title="Template talk:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Physical cosmology</b> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> concerned with the study of cosmological models.  A <b>cosmological model</b>, or simply <b>cosmology</b>, provides a description of the largest-scale structures and dynamics of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> and allows study of fundamental questions about its <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">origin</a>, structure, <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe" title="Chronology of the universe">evolution</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultimate_fate" title="Ultimate fate">ultimate fate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GFR_Ellis_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-GFR_Ellis-1">[1]</a></sup>  Cosmology as a <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> originated with the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_principle" title="Copernican principle">Copernican principle</a>, which implies that <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">celestial bodies</a> obey identical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical laws</a> to those on Earth, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_mechanics" title="Newtonian mechanics">Newtonian mechanics</a>, which first allowed those physical laws to be understood.
</p><p>Physical cosmology, as it is now understood, began with the development in 1915 of <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general theory of relativity</a>, followed by major observational discoveries in the 1920s: first, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> discovered that the universe contains a huge number of external <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> beyond the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>; then, work by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesto_Slipher" title="Vesto Slipher">Vesto Slipher</a> and others showed that the universe is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expanding_universe" title="Expanding universe">expanding</a>. These advances made it possible to speculate about the origin of the universe, and allowed the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory, by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lemaître">Georges Lemaître</a>, as the leading cosmological model. A few researchers still advocate a handful of <a href="/wiki/Non-standard_cosmology" title="Non-standard cosmology">alternative cosmologies</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> however, most cosmologists agree that the Big Bang theory best explains the observations.
</p><p>Dramatic advances in observational cosmology since the 1990s, including the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>, distant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernovae" title="Supernovae">supernovae</a> and galaxy <a href="/wiki/Redshift_survey" title="Redshift survey">redshift surveys</a>, have led to the development of a <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">standard model of cosmology</a>. This model requires the universe to contain large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a> whose nature is currently not well understood, but the model gives detailed predictions that are in excellent agreement with many diverse observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDG_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDG-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cosmology draws heavily on the work of many disparate areas of research in <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Applied_physics" title="Applied physics">applied physics</a>. Areas relevant to cosmology include <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> experiments and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particle_physics_phenomenology" title="Particle physics phenomenology">theory</a>, theoretical and observational <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" title="Plasma physics">plasma physics</a>.
</p>

Centauri</a></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table><table role="presentation" explosion</a></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table><table role="presentation" style="z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;display:block;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">Earliest mammals</a></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table><table role="presentation" style="position:absolute;z-index:15;top:0.091em;left:0em;transform:translateY(-50%);padding:0;margin:0;font-size:100%"><tbody><tr><td style="padding:0;text-align:left;vertical-align:middle"><span style="color:black">←</span></td><td style="padding:0;text-align:left;vertical-align:middle"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;top:0em"><div style="z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;display:block;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><a href="/wiki/Ape" title="Ape">Earliest apes</a> / <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:3.627em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:1.25em;left:-16.4em;width:8.8em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="display:block; text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Timeline of the evolutionary history of life">L<br/>i<br/>f<br/>e</a></span></div></div></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" id="Caption" style="padding:0;margin:0 0.2em 0.7em 0.2em"><div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em;border-style:none;clear:both;text-align:center;width:26.8em"><div style="float: left;">(<a href="/wiki/Billion_years" title="Billion years">billion years ago</a>)</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_cosmology" title="Timeline of cosmology">Timeline of cosmology</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_cosmologists" title="List of cosmologists">List of cosmologists</a></div>
<p>Modern cosmology developed along tandem tracks of theory and observation. In 1916, Albert Einstein published his theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, which provided a unified description of gravity as a geometric property of space and time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> At the time, Einstein believed in a <a href="/wiki/Static_universe" title="Static universe">static universe</a>, but found that his original formulation of the theory did not permit it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liddle,_A._51_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liddle,_A._51-5">[5]</a></sup> This is because masses distributed throughout the universe gravitationally attract, and move toward each other over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> However, he realized that his equations permitted the introduction of a constant term which could counteract the attractive force of gravity on the cosmic scale. Einstein published his first paper on relativistic cosmology in 1917, in which he added this <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a></i> to his field equations in order to force them to model a static universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The Einstein model describes a static universe; space is finite and unbounded (analogous to the surface of a sphere, which has a finite area but no edges). However, this so-called Einstein model is unstable to small perturbations—it will eventually start to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expand</a> or contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liddle,_A._51_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Liddle,_A._51-5">[5]</a></sup> It was later realized that Einstein's model was just one of a larger set of possibilities, all of which were consistent with general relativity and the cosmological principle. The cosmological solutions of general relativity were found by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedmann" title="Alexander Friedmann">Alexander Friedmann</a> in the early 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> His equations describe the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker" title="Friedmann–Lemaître–Robertson–Walker">Friedmann–Lemaître–Robertson–Walker</a> universe, which may expand or contract, and whose geometry may be open, flat, or closed.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:History_of_the_Universe.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/History_of_the_Universe.svg/350px-History_of_the_Universe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/History_of_the_Universe.svg/525px-History_of_the_Universe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/History_of_the_Universe.svg/700px-History_of_the_Universe.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:History_of_the_Universe.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>History of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> – <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a> are hypothesized to arise from <a href="/wiki/Inflation_(cosmology)" title="Inflation (cosmology)">cosmic inflation</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Faster-than-light#Universal_expansion" title="Faster-than-light">faster-than-light</a> expansion just after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BICEP2-2014_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-BICEP2-2014-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140317_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140317-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140317_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140317-11">[11]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1910s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesto_Melvin_Slipher" title="Vesto Melvin Slipher">Vesto Slipher</a> (and later <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Wirtz" title="Carl Wilhelm Wirtz">Carl Wilhelm Wirtz</a>) interpreted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_shift" title="Red shift">red shift</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">spiral nebulae</a> as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> that indicated they were receding from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, it is difficult to determine the distance to astronomical objects. One way is to compare the physical size of an object to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_size" title="Angular size">angular size</a>, but a physical size must be assumed to do this. Another method is to measure the <a href="/wiki/Brightness" title="Brightness">brightness</a> of an object and assume an intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a>, from which the distance may be determined using the <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">inverse-square law</a>. Due to the difficulty of using these methods, they did not realize that the nebulae were actually galaxies outside our own <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, nor did they speculate about the cosmological implications. In 1927, the <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priest</a> <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lemaître">Georges Lemaître</a> independently derived the Friedmann–Lemaître–Robertson–Walker equations and proposed, on the basis of the recession of spiral nebulae, that the universe began with the "explosion" of a "primeval <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>—which was later called the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. In 1929, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> provided an observational basis for Lemaître's theory. Hubble showed that the spiral nebulae were galaxies by determining their distances using measurements of the brightness of <a href="/wiki/Cepheid_variable" title="Cepheid variable">Cepheid variable</a> stars. He discovered a relationship between the redshift of a galaxy and its distance. He interpreted this as evidence that the galaxies are receding from Earth in every direction at speeds proportional to their distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> This fact is now known as <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble's law">Hubble's law</a>, though the numerical factor Hubble found relating recessional velocity and distance was off by a factor of ten, due to not knowing about the types of Cepheid variables.
</p><p>Given the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_principle" title="Cosmological principle">cosmological principle</a>, Hubble's law suggested that the universe was expanding. Two primary explanations were proposed for the expansion. One was Lemaître's Big Bang theory, advocated and developed by George Gamow. The other explanation was <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steady_state_model" title="Steady state model">steady state model</a> in which new matter is created as the galaxies move away from each other. In this model, the universe is roughly the same at any point in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aip_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-aip-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>For a number of years, support for these theories was evenly divided. However, the observational evidence began to support the idea that the universe evolved from a hot dense state. The discovery of the cosmic microwave background in 1965 lent strong support to the Big Bang model,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aip_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-aip-17">[17]</a></sup> and since the precise measurements of the cosmic microwave background by the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer" title="Cosmic Background Explorer">Cosmic Background Explorer</a> in the early 1990s, few cosmologists have seriously proposed other theories of the origin and evolution of the cosmos. One consequence of this is that in standard general relativity, the universe began with a <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">singularity</a>, as demonstrated by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Roger Penrose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earman1999_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Earman1999-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative view to extend the Big Bang model, suggesting the universe had no beginning or singularity and the age of the universe is infinite, has been presented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20150226_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20150226-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PL-20150204_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-PL-20150204-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR-20141118_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-AR-20141118-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_of_the_cosmos">Energy of the cosmos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Energy of the cosmos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The lightest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_elements" title="Chemical elements">chemical elements</a>, primarily <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, were created during the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> through the process of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burles2001_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burles2001-22">[22]</a></sup> In a sequence of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> reactions, smaller atomic nuclei are then combined into larger atomic nuclei, ultimately forming stable <a href="/wiki/Iron_group" title="Iron group">iron group</a> elements such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, which have the highest nuclear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binding_energies" title="Binding energies">binding energies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B2FH_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-B2FH-23">[23]</a></sup> The net process results in a <i>later energy release</i>, meaning subsequent to the Big Bang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frautschi1982_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frautschi1982-24">[24]</a></sup> Such reactions of nuclear particles can lead to <i>sudden energy releases</i> from <a href="/wiki/Cataclysmic_variable_star" title="Cataclysmic variable star">cataclysmic variable stars</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a>. Gravitational collapse of matter into <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a> also powers the most energetic processes, generally seen in the nuclear regions of galaxies, forming <i><a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_galaxies" title="Active galaxies">active galaxies</a></i>.
</p><p>Cosmologists cannot explain all cosmic phenomena exactly, such as those related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">accelerating expansion of the universe</a>, using conventional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_forms" title="Energy forms">forms of energy</a>. Instead, cosmologists propose a new form of energy called <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a> that permeates all space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> One hypothesis is that dark energy is just the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a>, a component of empty space that is associated with the <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual particles</a> that exist due to the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frieman2008_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frieman2008-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no clear way to define the total energy in the universe using the most widely accepted theory of gravity, <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>. Therefore, it remains controversial whether the total energy is conserved in an expanding universe. For instance, each <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> that travels through intergalactic space loses energy due to the <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> effect. This energy is not obviously transferred to any other system, so seems to be permanently lost. On the other hand, some cosmologists insist that energy is conserved in some sense; this follows the law of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different forms of energy may dominate the cosmos – <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_particle" title="Relativistic particle">relativistic particles</a> which are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>, or non-relativistic particles referred to as matter. Relativistic particles are particles whose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> is zero or negligible compared to their <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, and so move at the speed of light or very close to it; non-relativistic particles have much higher rest mass than their energy and so move much slower than the speed of light.
</p><p>As the universe expands, both matter and radiation become diluted. However, the <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy densities</a> of radiation and matter dilute at different rates. As a particular volume expands, mass-energy density is changed only by the increase in volume, but the energy density of radiation is changed both by the increase in volume and by the increase in the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> that make it up. Thus the energy of radiation becomes a smaller part of the universe's total energy than that of matter as it expands. The very early universe is said to have been 'radiation dominated' and radiation controlled the deceleration of expansion. Later, as the average energy per photon becomes roughly 10 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a> and lower, matter dictates the rate of deceleration and the universe is said to be 'matter dominated'. The intermediate case is not treated well <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analytic_solution" title="Analytic solution">analytically</a>. As the expansion of the universe continues, matter dilutes even further and the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> becomes dominant, leading to an acceleration in the universe's expansion.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_universe">History of the universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History of the universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">Timeline of the Big Bang</a></div>
<p>The history of the universe is a central issue in cosmology. The history of the universe is divided into different periods called epochs, according to the dominant forces and processes in each period. The standard cosmological model is known as the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equations_of_motion">Equations of motion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Equations of motion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann–Lemaître–Robertson–Walker metric">Friedmann–Lemaître–Robertson–Walker metric</a></div>
<p>Within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cosmological_model" title="Standard cosmological model">standard cosmological model</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Equations_of_motion" title="Equations of motion">equations of motion</a> governing the universe as a whole are derived from <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> with a small, positive <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The solution is an expanding universe; due to this expansion, the radiation and matter in the universe cool down and become diluted. At first, the expansion is slowed down by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> attracting the <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> and matter in the universe. However, as these become diluted, the cosmological constant becomes more dominant and the expansion of the universe starts to accelerate rather than decelerate. In our universe this happened billions of years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Springel2006_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Springel2006-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particle_physics_in_cosmology">Particle physics in cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Particle physics in cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics_in_cosmology" title="Particle physics in cosmology">Particle physics in cosmology</a></div>
<p>During the earliest moments of the universe, the average <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> was very high, making knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> critical to understanding this environment. Hence, <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> processes and <a href="/wiki/Particle_decay" title="Particle decay">decay</a> of unstable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elementary_particles" title="Elementary particles">elementary particles</a> are important for cosmological models of this period.
</p><p>As a rule of thumb, a scattering or a decay process is cosmologically important in a certain epoch if the time scale describing that process is smaller than, or comparable to, the time scale of the expansion of the universe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Isn't the opposite true – departures from equilibrium occur only when the process takes longer than the time scale? (April 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The time scale that describes the expansion of the universe is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 1/H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 1/H}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="1/H" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2376a7cc16c82d6d771762914d9ff5981a942c87" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.389ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> with <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

The expansion timescale <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 1/H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang">Timeline of the Big Bang</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Timeline of the Big Bang">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">Timeline of the Big Bang</a></div>
<p>Observations suggest that the universe began around 13.8 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Since then, the evolution of the universe has passed through three phases. The very early universe, which is still poorly understood, was the split second in which the universe was so hot that <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">particles</a> had energies higher than those currently accessible in <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a> on Earth. Therefore, while the basic features of this epoch have been worked out in the Big Bang theory, the details are largely based on educated guesses.
Following this, in the early universe, the evolution of the universe proceeded according to known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_energy_physics" title="High energy physics">high energy physics</a>. This is when the first protons, electrons and neutrons formed, then nuclei and finally atoms. With the formation of neutral hydrogen, the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> was emitted. Finally, the epoch of structure formation began, when matter started to aggregate into the first <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a>, and ultimately galaxies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clusters_of_galaxies" title="Clusters of galaxies">clusters of galaxies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">superclusters</a> formed. The future of the universe is not yet firmly known, but according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9BCDM" title="ΛCDM">ΛCDM</a> model it will continue expanding forever.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Areas_of_study">Areas of study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Areas of study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Below, some of the most active areas of inquiry in cosmology are described, in roughly chronological order. This does not include all of the Big Bang cosmology, which is presented in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">Timeline of the Big Bang</a>.</i>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Very_early_universe">Very early universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Very early universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The early, hot universe appears to be well explained by the Big Bang from roughly 10<sup>−33</sup> seconds onwards, but there are several <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang#Problems" title="Big Bang">problems</a>. One is that there is no compelling reason, using current particle physics, for the universe to be <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">flat</a>, homogeneous, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> <i>(see the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_principle" title="Cosmological principle">cosmological principle</a>)</i>. Moreover, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_unified_theory" title="Grand unified theory">grand unified theories</a> of particle physics suggest that there should be <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">magnetic monopoles</a> in the universe, which have not been found. These problems are resolved by a brief period of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">cosmic inflation</a>, which drives the universe to <a href="/wiki/Flatness_(cosmology)" title="Flatness (cosmology)">flatness</a>, smooths out <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropies</a> and inhomogeneities to the observed level, and exponentially dilutes the monopoles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guth1981_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guth1981-31">[31]</a></sup> The physical model behind cosmic inflation is extremely simple, but it has not yet been confirmed by particle physics, and there are difficult problems reconciling inflation and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (April 2018)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> Some cosmologists think that <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brane_cosmology" title="Brane cosmology">brane cosmology</a> will provide an alternative to inflation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pogosian2003_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pogosian2003-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another major problem in cosmology is what caused the universe to contain far more matter than <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>. Cosmologists can observationally deduce that the universe is not split into regions of matter and antimatter. If it were, there would be <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> produced as a result of <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilation</a>, but this is not observed. Therefore, some process in the early universe must have created a small excess of matter over antimatter, and this (currently not understood) process is called <i><a href="/wiki/Baryogenesis" title="Baryogenesis">baryogenesis</a></i>. Three required conditions for baryogenesis were derived by <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Sakharov" title="Andrei Sakharov">Andrei Sakharov</a> in 1967, and requires a violation of the particle physics <a href="/wiki/Symmetry#In_physics" title="Symmetry">symmetry</a>, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CP-symmetry" title="CP-symmetry">CP-symmetry</a>, between matter and antimatter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canetti2012_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canetti2012-33">[33]</a></sup> However, particle accelerators measure too small a violation of CP-symmetry to account for the baryon asymmetry. Cosmologists and particle physicists look for additional violations of the CP-symmetry in the early universe that might account for the baryon asymmetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pandolfi2017_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pandolfi2017-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both the problems of baryogenesis and cosmic inflation are very closely related to particle physics, and their resolution might come from high energy theory and <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">experiment</a>, rather than through observations of the universe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#CRYSTAL" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not"><span title="Material appears to be speculative in nature. (April 2018)">speculation?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Bang_Theory">Big Bang Theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Big Bang Theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a></div>
<p>Big Bang nucleosynthesis is the theory of the formation of the elements in the early universe. It finished when the universe was about three minutes old and its <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> dropped below that at which <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> could occur. Big Bang nucleosynthesis had a brief period during which it could operate, so only the very lightest elements were produced. Starting from <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> (<a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>), it principally produced <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium-4</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>. Other elements were produced in only trace abundances. The basic theory of nucleosynthesis was developed in 1948 by <a href="/wiki/George_Gamow" title="George Gamow">George Gamow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Asher_Alpher" title="Ralph Asher Alpher">Ralph Asher Alpher</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Herman" title="Robert Herman">Robert Herman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peebles2014_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peebles2014-35">[35]</a></sup> It was used for many years as a probe of physics at the time of the Big Bang, as the theory of Big Bang nucleosynthesis connects the abundances of primordial light elements with the features of the early universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burles2001_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burles2001-22">[22]</a></sup> Specifically, it can be used to test the <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boucher2004_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boucher2004-36">[36]</a></sup> to probe <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, and test <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a> physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cyburt2016_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cyburt2016-37">[37]</a></sup> Some cosmologists have proposed that Big Bang nucleosynthesis suggests there is a fourth "sterile" species of neutrino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucente2018_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucente2018-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_model_of_Big_Bang_cosmology">Standard model of Big Bang cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Standard model of Big Bang cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <b>ΛCDM</b> (<b>Lambda cold dark matter</b>) or <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM" title="Lambda-CDM">Lambda-CDM</a></b> model is a <a href="/wiki/Parametric_equation" title="Parametric equation">parametrization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> cosmological model in which the universe contains a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>, denoted by <a href="/wiki/Lambda" title="Lambda">Lambda</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek</a> <b>Λ</b>), associated with <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> (abbreviated <b>CDM</b>). It is frequently referred to as the <b>standard model</b> of <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> cosmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck2015_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck2015-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlisle_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carlisle-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_microwave_background">Cosmic microwave background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cosmic microwave background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">Cosmic microwave background</a></div>
<p>The cosmic microwave background is radiation left over from <a href="/wiki/Decoupling_(cosmology)" title="Decoupling (cosmology)">decoupling</a> after the epoch of <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination</a> when neutral <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> first formed. At this point, radiation produced in the Big Bang stopped <a href="/wiki/Thomson_scattering" title="Thomson scattering">Thomson scattering</a> from charged ions. The radiation, first observed in 1965 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arno_Penzias" title="Arno Penzias">Arno Penzias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Woodrow_Wilson" title="Robert Woodrow Wilson">Robert Woodrow Wilson</a>, has a perfect thermal <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black-body</a> spectrum. It has a temperature of 2.7 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a> today and is isotropic to one part in 10<sup>5</sup>. <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_perturbation_theory" title="Cosmological perturbation theory">Cosmological perturbation theory</a>, which describes the evolution of slight inhomogeneities in the early universe, has allowed cosmologists to precisely calculate the angular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_spectrum" title="Power spectrum">power spectrum</a> of the radiation, and it has been measured by the recent satellite experiments (<a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer" title="Cosmic Background Explorer">COBE</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamarre2010_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lamarre2010-41">[41]</a></sup> and many ground and balloon-based experiments (such as <a href="/wiki/Degree_Angular_Scale_Interferometer" title="Degree Angular Scale Interferometer">Degree Angular Scale Interferometer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Imager" title="Cosmic Background Imager">Cosmic Background Imager</a>, and <a href="/wiki/BOOMERanG_experiment" title="BOOMERanG experiment">Boomerang</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sievers2003_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sievers2003-42">[42]</a></sup> One of the goals of these efforts is to measure the basic parameters of the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a> with increasing accuracy, as well as to test the predictions of the Big Bang model and look for new physics. The results of measurements made by WMAP, for example, have placed limits on the neutrino masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hinshaw2013_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hinshaw2013-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newer experiments, such as <a href="/wiki/QUIET" title="QUIET">QUIET</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Cosmology_Telescope" title="Atacama Cosmology Telescope">Atacama Cosmology Telescope</a>, are trying to measure the <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> of the cosmic microwave background.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naess2014_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naess2014-44">[44]</a></sup> These measurements are expected to provide further confirmation of the theory as well as information about cosmic inflation, and the so-called secondary anisotropies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson2009_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson2009-45">[45]</a></sup> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunyaev-Zel%27dovich_effect" title="Sunyaev-Zel'dovich effect">Sunyaev-Zel'dovich effect</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sachs-Wolfe_effect" title="Sachs-Wolfe effect">Sachs-Wolfe effect</a>, which are caused by interaction between <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">clusters</a> with the cosmic microwave background.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scranton2003_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scranton2003-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Refregier1999_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Refregier1999-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 March 2014, astronomers of the <a href="/wiki/BICEP_and_Keck_Array#BICEP2" title="BICEP and Keck Array">BICEP2 Collaboration</a> announced the apparent detection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-modes" title="B-modes"><i>B</i>-mode</a> <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> of the CMB, considered to be evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primordial_gravitational_wave" title="Primordial gravitational wave">primordial gravitational waves</a> that are predicted by the theory of <a href="/wiki/Inflation_(cosmology)" title="Inflation (cosmology)">inflation</a> to occur during the earliest phase of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BICEP2-2014_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-BICEP2-2014-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140317_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140317-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140317_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140317-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140324_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140324-48">[48]</a></sup> However, later that year the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_spacecraft" title="Planck spacecraft">Planck</a> collaboration provided a more accurate measurement of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a>, concluding that the B-mode signal from dust is the same strength as that reported from BICEP2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AXV-20140919_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-AXV-20140919-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140922_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140922-50">[50]</a></sup> On 30 January 2015, a joint analysis of BICEP2 and <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)" title="Planck (spacecraft)">Planck</a> data was published and the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> announced that the signal can be entirely attributed to interstellar dust in the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature-20150130_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature-20150130-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution_of_large-scale_structure">Formation and evolution of large-scale structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Formation and evolution of large-scale structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">Large-scale structure of the cosmos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">Structure formation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">Galaxy formation and evolution</a></div>
<p>Understanding the formation and evolution of the largest and earliest structures (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">clusters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">superclusters</a>) is one of the largest efforts in cosmology. Cosmologists study a model of <b>hierarchical structure formation</b> in which structures form from the bottom up, with smaller objects forming first, while the largest objects, such as superclusters, are still assembling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hess2013_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hess2013-52">[52]</a></sup> One way to study structure in the universe is to survey the visible galaxies, in order to construct a three-dimensional picture of the galaxies in the universe and measure the matter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_spectrum" title="Power spectrum">power spectrum</a>. This is the approach of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">Sloan Digital Sky Survey</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/2dF_Galaxy_Redshift_Survey" title="2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey">2dF Galaxy Redshift Survey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cole2005_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cole2005-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nichol2007_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nichol2007-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another tool for understanding structure formation is simulations, which cosmologists use to study the gravitational aggregation of matter in the universe, as it clusters into <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">filaments</a>, superclusters and <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a>. Most simulations contain only non-baryonic <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a>, which should suffice to understand the universe on the largest scales, as there is much more dark matter in the universe than visible, baryonic matter. More advanced simulations are starting to include baryons and study the formation of individual galaxies. Cosmologists study these simulations to see if they agree with the galaxy surveys, and to understand any discrepancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhlen2012_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhlen2012-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other, complementary observations to measure the distribution of matter in the distant universe and to probe <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">reionization</a> include:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Lyman-alpha_forest" title="Lyman-alpha forest">Lyman-alpha forest</a>, which allows cosmologists to measure the distribution of neutral atomic hydrogen gas in the early universe, by measuring the absorption of light from distant quasars by the gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberg2003_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberg2003-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">21-centimeter</a> <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorption</a> line of neutral atomic hydrogen also provides a sensitive test of cosmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furlanetto2006_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furlanetto2006-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_lensing" title="Weak lensing">Weak lensing</a>, the distortion of a distant image by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a> due to dark matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munshi2008_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munshi2008-58">[58]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>These will help cosmologists settle the question of when and how structure formed in the universe.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_matter">Dark matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dark matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a></div>
<p>Evidence from <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>, structure formation, and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">galaxy rotation curves</a> suggests that about 23% of the mass of the universe consists of non-baryonic dark matter, whereas only 4% consists of visible, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic_matter" title="Baryonic matter">baryonic matter</a>. The gravitational effects of dark matter are well understood, as it behaves like a cold, <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">non-radiative</a> fluid that forms <a href="/wiki/Galactic_halo" title="Galactic halo">haloes</a> around galaxies. Dark matter has never been detected in the laboratory, and the particle physics nature of dark matter remains completely unknown. Without observational constraints, there are a number of candidates, such as a stable <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetric</a> particle, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particle" title="Weakly interacting massive particle">weakly interacting massive particle</a>, a gravitationally-interacting massive particle, an <a href="/wiki/Axion" title="Axion">axion</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Massive_compact_halo_object" title="Massive compact halo object">massive compact halo object</a>. Alternatives to the dark matter hypothesis include a modification of gravity at small accelerations (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOND" title="MOND">MOND</a>) or an effect from <a href="/wiki/Brane_cosmology" title="Brane cosmology">brane cosmology</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TeVeS" title="TeVeS">TeVeS</a> is a version of MOND that can explain gravitational lensing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klasen2015_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klasen2015-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_energy">Dark energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Dark energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a></div>
<p>If the universe is <a href="/wiki/Flatness_(cosmology)" title="Flatness (cosmology)">flat</a>, there must be an additional component making up 73% (in addition to the 23% dark matter and 4% baryons) of the energy density of the universe. This is called dark energy. In order not to interfere with Big Bang nucleosynthesis and the cosmic microwave background, it must not cluster in haloes like baryons and dark matter. There is strong observational evidence for dark energy, as the total energy density of the universe is known through constraints on the flatness of the universe, but the amount of clustering matter is tightly measured, and is much less than this. The case for dark energy was strengthened in 1999, when measurements demonstrated that the expansion of the universe has begun to gradually accelerate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perlmutter1999_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perlmutter1999-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from its density and its clustering properties, nothing is known about dark energy. <i><a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">Quantum field theory</a></i> predicts a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> (CC) much like dark energy, but 120 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude" title="Orders of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> larger than that observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adler1995_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adler1995-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steven_Weinberg" title="Steven Weinberg">Steven Weinberg</a> and a number of string theorists <i>(see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/String_landscape" title="String landscape">string landscape</a>)</i> have invoked the 'weak <a href="/wiki/Anthropic_principle" title="Anthropic principle">anthropic principle</a>': i.e. the reason that physicists observe a universe with such a small cosmological constant is that no physicists (or any life) could exist in a universe with a larger cosmological constant. Many cosmologists find this an unsatisfying explanation: perhaps because while the weak anthropic principle is self-evident (given that living observers exist, there must be at least one universe with a cosmological constant which allows for life to exist) it does not attempt to explain the context of that universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegfried2006_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siegfried2006-62">[62]</a></sup> For example, the weak anthropic principle alone does not distinguish between:
</p>
<ul><li>Only one universe will ever exist and there is some underlying principle that constrains the CC to the value we observe.</li>
<li>Only one universe will ever exist and although there is no underlying principle fixing the CC, we got lucky.</li>
<li>Lots of universes exist (simultaneously or serially) with a range of CC values, and of course ours is one of the life-supporting ones.</li></ul>
<p>Other possible explanations for dark energy include <a href="/wiki/Quintessence_(physics)" title="Quintessence (physics)">quintessence</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahni2002_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahni2002-63">[63]</a></sup> or a modification of gravity on the largest scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nojiri2007_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nojiri2007-64">[64]</a></sup> The effect on cosmology of the dark energy that these models describe is given by the dark energy's <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state_(cosmology)" title="Equation of state (cosmology)">equation of state</a>, which varies depending upon the theory. The nature of dark energy is one of the most challenging problems in cosmology.
</p><p>A better understanding of dark energy is likely to solve the problem of the <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_fate_of_the_universe" title="Ultimate fate of the universe">ultimate fate of the universe</a>. In the current cosmological epoch, the accelerated expansion due to dark energy is preventing structures larger than <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">superclusters</a> from forming. It is not known whether the acceleration will continue indefinitely, perhaps even increasing until a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_rip" title="Big rip">big rip</a>, or whether it will eventually reverse, lead to a <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Big_freeze" title="Big freeze">big freeze</a>, or follow some other scenario.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jambrina2014_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jambrina2014-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_waves">Gravitational waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gravitational waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">Gravitational waves</a>  are ripples in the <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curvature</a> of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> that propagate as <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> at the speed of light, generated in certain gravitational interactions that propagate outward from their source. <a href="/wiki/Gravitational-wave_astronomy" title="Gravitational-wave astronomy">Gravitational-wave astronomy</a> is an emerging branch of <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">observational astronomy</a> which aims to use gravitational waves to collect observational data about sources of detectable gravitational waves such as <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> systems composed of <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>; and events such as <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>, and the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe" title="Chronology of the universe">early universe</a> shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colpi2017_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colpi2017-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> Scientific Collaboration and <a href="/wiki/Virgo_interferometer" title="Virgo interferometer">Virgo</a> Collaboration teams announced that they had made the <a href="/wiki/First_observation_of_gravitational_waves" title="First observation of gravitational waves">first observation of gravitational waves</a>, originating from a <a href="/wiki/Binary_black_hole" title="Binary black hole">pair</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_collision" title="Stellar collision">merging</a> black holes using the Advanced LIGO detectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2016_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discovery_2016-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbot_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbot-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-69">[69]</a></sup> On 15 June 2016, a <a href="/wiki/GW151226" title="GW151226">second detection</a> of gravitational waves from coalescing black holes was announced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160615_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160615-70">[70]</a></sup> Besides LIGO, many other <a href="/wiki/Gravitational-wave_observatory" title="Gravitational-wave observatory">gravitational-wave observatories (detectors)</a> are under construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_areas_of_inquiry">Other areas of inquiry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other areas of inquiry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cosmologists also study:
</p>
<ul><li>Whether <a href="/wiki/Primordial_black_hole" title="Primordial black hole">primordial black holes</a> were formed in our universe, and what happened to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kovetz2017_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kovetz2017-72">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>Detection of cosmic rays with energies above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GZK_cutoff" title="GZK cutoff">GZK cutoff</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takeda1998_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Takeda1998-73">[73]</a></sup> and whether it signals a failure of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> at high energies.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boucher2004_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boucher2004-36">[36]</a></sup> whether or not Einstein's <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general theory of relativity</a> is the correct theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turyshev2008_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turyshev2008-74">[74]</a></sup> and if the fundamental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laws_of_physics" title="Laws of physics">laws of physics</a> are the same everywhere in the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uzan2011_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uzan2011-75">[75]</a></sup></li></ul>
